Rational Arguments Classified: The Primacy of Burhan for Creed
Sanusi classifies rational arguments into three types and identifies burhan as the one relied upon for creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's classification of rational arguments into burhan, khitaba/amara, and mughalata, and why only burhan (certain demonstration) is relied upon for establishing religious beliefs.

Core Idea
Only certain rational proof (burhan) can ground creed; probable or fallacious arguments have no place.

Sanusi introduces the fundamental classification of rational proofs from al-Baydawi. He distinguishes three types based on the certainty of their premises. Understanding this taxonomy is crucial because it determines which arguments are valid for theology. Sanusi then clarifies his own terminology and explains why he uses specific qualifiers for the proofs he relies on.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis to get its flow. Then read the translation to understand the three divisions and their basis.
Notice the reference to al-Baydawi's al-Tawali' as the source of this classification.
2
Identify the three types of rational arguments
Find the three types: burhan, khitaba/amara, and mughalata. Note the criterion for division: the certainty of premises (certain, probable, or resembling).
The first type is called both burhan and dalil; the second is called khitaba and amara.
3
Understand which type is authoritative for creed
Sanusi states that only the first type (burhan) is relied upon for correcting religious beliefs. Ask yourself why probable or fallacious arguments are insufficient.
This is the core methodological point of the passage.
4
Examine Sanusi's explanation of his phrasing
He explains why he used 'min al-barahin al-qati'a' and attached 'al-adilla' as a general term. Note the conditions for accepting rational proofs: they must be certain and pertain to matters not dependent on miracles.
He also adds the qualifier 'al-sati'a' to emphasize certainty.
5
Consider the alternative phrasing he suggests
Sanusi suggests it would be clearer to say 'min al-barahin al-aqliyya wa al-qawati' al-sam'iyya'. Reflect on why this might be an improvement.
The passage ends abruptly; the next page likely continues this thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه أقسام الحُجَّةِ العقلية وجعلها البيضاوي في ( الطوالع ، ثلاثة أقسام : البرهان ، والخطابةُ وتُسمَّى أيضاً الأمارة ، والمغالطة ؛ لأنَّ الحُجَّةَ العقليَّةَ : إِمَّا . أنْ تَتركَّبَ مِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ قطعيَّةٍ ، أو مِنْ مُقدِّماتِ ظنيَّةٍ ، أو مِنْ شبيهة بأحداهما ، وتُسمَّى الأولى : برهاناً ودليلاً ، والثانية : خطابةً وأمارة ، والثالثة : مغالطة (۱) (۳) ; وبالجملة : فالمعتمدُ مِنْ هذهِ الأقسام في تصحيح العقائدِ الدينية : القسم الأوّلُ الذي هو البرهان ، فلذا قلت : ( مِنَ البراهين ) ووصفتها بـ ( القاطعة ( لكشفِ معناها (٢) ، وإنَّما عطفتُ عليها ( الأدلَّةِ ) عطف عام على خاص ؛ لتدخل في ذلك الأدلَّةُ العقليَّةُ فيما تُقبَلُ فيهِ مِنَ العقائدِ ؛ وذلكَ كلُّ ما لا تَتوقَّفُ المعجزة عليهِ كنفي النقائص عنه تعالى ، وثبوتِ الوحدانية له على رأي ، وكوقوعِ بعض الممكناتِ مِنَ الحشرِ والرؤيةِ ونحوهما ، ووصفتها بالسطوع إشارة إلى اشتراط القطع فيها أيضاً ، ولو كان بدل هذا الكلام يُقالَ : ( مِنَ البراهين العقلية والقواطع السمعية ).. لكان أبينَ وأحسن أَنَّ
These are the divisions of the rational proof. And al-Baydawi in al-Tawali' divided them into three divisions: Burhan (demonstration), Khitaba (rhetoric) also called Amara (indication), and Mughalata (fallacy); because rational proof is either composed of certain premises, or of probable premises, or of something resembling one of them. The first is called Burhan and Dalil (proof), the second is called Khitaba and Amara, the third is called Mughalata. In summary: the relied-upon of these divisions in correcting religious beliefs is the first division, which is Burhan. Therefore I said: "from the Burhans (al-barahin)" and qualified them with "al-qati'a (the decisive)" to uncover its meaning. And I only attached "al-adilla (the proofs)" by conjoining general to specific, so that rational proofs may enter in those [beliefs] in which they are accepted from the beliefs. That is everything on which the miracle does not depend, such as negating imperfections from Him the Most High, and establishing oneness for Him according to an opinion, and like the occurrence of some possible things from the Resurrection and the Vision and the like. And I qualified them with "al-sati'a (the brilliant)" as an indication of the condition of certainty in them as well. And if instead of this statement one said: "from the rational proofs and the scriptural certainties (al-qawati' al-sam'iyya)" it would be clearer and better, that...
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a classification of rational arguments into three types, following al-Baydawi's classification in al-Tawali'. The three types are: burhan (demonstration) based on certain premises, khitaba or amara (rhetoric/indication) based on probable premises, and mughalata (fallacy) based on premises that resemble certain or probable premises but are not. The author states that the only division relied upon for correcting religious beliefs is the first, burhan. He then explains his own phrasing: he used 'al-barahin al-qati'a' (decisive demonstrations) and added 'al-adilla' (proofs) as a general term to include rational proofs in those beliefs that do not depend on miracles, such as negating imperfections from Allah and establishing His oneness. He qualifies 'al-adilla' with 'al-sati'a' (brilliant) to indicate that these proofs must also be certain. Finally, he suggests that it would have been clearer to say 'al-barahin al-'aqliyya wa al-qawati' al-sam'iyya' (rational demonstrations and scriptural certainties).

Key Terms
الحجة العقلية (hujja 'aqliyya)
البرهان (burhan)
الخطابة (khitaba)
الأمارة (amara)
المغالطة (mughalata)
القواطع السمعية (qawati' sam'iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think any rational argument is sufficient for creed, including those based on probable premises or rhetoric.
Correction: Sanusi limits valid rational arguments for creed to burhan, which uses certain premises. Probable (khitaba/amara) or fallacious (mughalata) arguments are not relied upon.

Checkpoint
Which type of rational argument is relied upon for correcting religious beliefs, according to Sanusi?
A) Khitaba
B) Mughalata
C) Burhan
D) Amara

Key Takeaway
In Islamic theology, only demonstrative arguments with certain premises (burhan) are valid for establishing creed; rhetorical or fallacious arguments are insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend your beliefs to others, do you rely on certain, well-grounded proofs or do you sometimes use merely persuasive arguments? How might that affect the strength of your faith?
?
Sanusi teaches that only certain proofs are acceptable for creed. Are there areas of your own faith where you have accepted uncertain or weak arguments? What would it mean for you to seek certain knowledge in those areas?
?
If you knew that a weak argument could lead someone to doubt the truth of Islam, would you still use it? Will you commit to only presenting the most certain proofs for the core beliefs of your faith?

Five Sources of Judgment and Valid Faith
Sanusi enumerates the five sources of judgment and applies them to faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five sources of originated judgment and how they determine the validity of faith according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
Faith must be based on certain knowledge, not imitation or conjecture.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of judgment types in Islamic theology. Sanusi begins by warning against blind imitation (taqlid) in creed, then presents a systematic analysis of how judgments arise in the mind — from certain knowledge to doubt — and applies this taxonomy to evaluate what constitutes valid faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the opening condemnation of taqlid and the subsequent classification of five judgment types.
2
Observe the derivation of the five types
Sanusi derives the five types by first asking whether a judgment is certain or not, and then further categorizing certainty and non-certainty. Trace this logical derivation in your mind.
3
Understand the application to faith
The passage concludes by applying the classification to faith: only the first type of certainty (knowledge) yields valid faith, while the three non-certain types do not. Reflect on why this is a strict criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على التقليد
ولا يرضى لعقائدِهِ حرفة التقليد ؛ فإِنَّهَا غيرُ مُخلّصةٍ في الآخرةِ عند كثير مِنَ المحققينَ
الحكم الحادث ينشأ عن أمورٍ خمسة ]
اعلم : أنَّ الحكم الحادث ينشأ عن أمور خمسة : علم ، واعتقاد ، وظن ، وشكٍّ ، ووهم ؛ لأنَّ الحاكم بأمرٍ على أمرٍ ثبوتاً أو نفياً : إمَّا أن يجد في نفسِهِ الجزم بذلك الحكم أو لا ، والأَوَّلُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ لسبب - وأعني به : ضرورةً أو برهاناً ـ أو لا ، وغيرُ الجزمِ : إِمَّا أَنْ يكون راجحاً على مقابله ، أو مرجوحاً ، أو مساوياً .
فأقسام الجزم اثنانِ ، وأقسام غير الجزم ثلاثة ، ويُسمَّى الأَوَّلُ مِنْ قسمي الجزم : علماً ومعرفةً ويقيناً ، والثاني : اعتقاداً ، ويُسمى الأَوَّلُ مِنْ أقسامِ غيرِ الجزمِ : ظناً ، والثاني : وهماً ، والثالث : شكاً .
معرفة الإيمان الصحيح مِنْ غيرِهِ ]
فإذا عرفت هذا : فالإيمان إن حصل عن أقسام غير الجزمِ الثلاثة.. فالإجماع على بطلانه ، وإن حصل عنِ القسم الأوَّلِ مِنْ قسمي الجزم ؛ وهو العلم .. فالإجماع على صحتِهِ ، وأما القسم
Discussion on Taqlid. And he is not satisfied for his creed with the craft of taqlid; for indeed it is not salvific in the afterlife according to many of the investigators. The originated judgment arises from five things: Know that the originated judgment arises from five things: knowledge, belief, opinion, doubt, and illusion; because the one who judges something positively or negatively either finds in himself certainty of that judgment or not. The first: either it is due to a cause—and I mean by it: necessity or demonstration—or not. And what is not certainty: either it is more preponderant than its opposite, or less preponderant, or equal. So the divisions of certainty are two, and the divisions of non-certainty are three. The first of the two divisions of certainty is called knowledge, cognition, and certitude; the second is called belief. The first of the divisions of non-certainty is called opinion, the second illusion, the third doubt. Knowing correct faith from other [types]: So if you have understood this: then faith, if it arises from the three divisions of non-certainty, there is consensus on its invalidity; and if it arises from the first of the two divisions of certainty, which is knowledge, there is consensus on its validity. As for the [second] division...
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of judgment (hukm) and an application to the evaluation of faith (iman). It begins by condemning taqlid (imitation) in matters of creed, stating that it is not salvific in the afterlife according to many scholars. Then it presents a fivefold classification of the sources of originated judgments: knowledge (ilm), belief (i'tiqad), opinion (zann), doubt (shakk), and illusion (wahm). The classification is derived by analyzing mental states of the judge: either he finds certainty (jazzm) or not. Certainty is further divided into that which has a cause (either necessity or demonstration) and that which does not (i.e., blind certainty). The latter is belief (i'tiqad). Non-certainty is divided into three: opinion (rajih), illusion (marjuh), and doubt (musawi). Thus, there are two kinds of certainty and three kinds of non-certainty, each given distinct names. The author then applies this to faith: if faith arises from any of the three non-certain types, consensus holds it invalid. If it arises from the first type of certainty (knowledge by necessity or demonstration), consensus holds it valid. The text breaks off at the discussion of the second type of certainty (belief).

Misconception: Some may think belief (اعتقاد) is sufficient for valid faith
Correction: The text indicates that only knowledge (علم) based on necessity or demonstration is acceptable; belief without such basis is not salvific.

Key Takeaway

The Five Types of Arising Judgment
Sanusi classifies the five types of arising judgment and their relationship to certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's classification of the five types of judgment and the distinction between certainty-based and non-certainty-based judgments.

Core Idea
All judgments are either certain or not; certainty has two sources, uncertainty has three degrees.

Sanusi begins his discussion of the validity of beliefs by categorizing the mental states that accompany any judgment. He identifies five possible types of judgment that arise in the mind when one affirms or denies something. This classification helps distinguish genuine knowledge from mere belief, assumption, doubt, and illusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to get the overall structure.
Pay attention to the opening remark about taqlid.
2
Focus on the initial remark about taqlid
Sanusi rejects blind following (taqlid) as acceptable for beliefs because it is not salvific according to many investigators. Note that this sets the stage for the classification.
3
Identify the five types of judgment
List the five types in order: knowledge ('ilm), belief (i'tiqad), assumption (zann), doubt (shakk), and illusion (wahm). Notice that they are presented as arising from the act of judging.
These correspond to different degrees of certainty.
4
Understand the division into certainty and non-certainty
Categories 1-2 are certain (jazm), 3-5 are not. Certainty is further divided: with a cause (necessity or proof) gives knowledge; without cause gives belief. Non-certainty is divided by preponderance, outweigh, or equality.
This hierarchy is foundational for later arguments about the necessity of proof for faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على التقليد ولا يرضى لعقائدِهِ حرفة التقليد ؛ فإِنَّهَا غيرُ مُخلّصةٍ في الآخرةِ عند كثير مِنَ المحققينَ الحكم الحادث ينشأ عن أمور خمسة اعلم : أنَّ الحكم الحادث ينشأ عن أمور خمسة : علم ، واعتقاد ، وظن ، وشكٍّ ، ووهم ؛ لأنَّ الحاكم بأمرٍ على أمرٍ ثبوتاً أو نفياً : إمَّا أن يجد في نفسِهِ الجزم بذلك الحكم أو لا ، والأَوَّلُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ لسبب - وأعني به : ضرورةً أو برهاناً ـ أو لا ، وغيرُ الجزمِ : إِمَّا أَنْ يكون راجحاً على مقابله ، أو مرجوحاً ، أو مساوياً . فأقسام الجزم اثنانِ ، وأقسام غير الجزم ثلاثة ، ويُسمَّى الأَوَّلُ مِنْ قسمي الجزم : علماً ومعرفةً ويقيناً ، والثاني : اعتقاداً ، ويُسمى الأَوَّلُ مِنْ أقسامِ غيرِ الجزمِ : ظناً ، والثاني : وهماً ، والثالث : شكاً .
Discourse on blind following (taqlid). He does not approve for his beliefs the craft of blind following; for it is not salvific in the afterlife according to many of the investigators (muhaqqiqun). The arising judgment (al-hukm al-hadith) arises from five things: know that the arising judgment arises from five things: knowledge ('ilm), belief (i'tiqad), assumption (zann), doubt (shakk), and illusion (wahm). Because the one who judges something as existent or non-existent: either he finds in himself certainty (jazm) regarding that judgment or not. The first: either it is due to a cause—and I mean by it: necessity or proof—or not. And non-certainty: either it is preponderant (rajih) over its opposite, or outweighed (marjuh), or equal (musawi). So the divisions of certainty are two, and the divisions of non-certainty are three. The first of the two divisions of certainty is called knowledge, cognition, and certainty; the second is called belief. The first of the divisions of non-certainty is called assumption; the second is called illusion; the third is called doubt.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage. Sanusi begins by introducing the topic of blind following (taqlid) and states that it is not acceptable for beliefs because it is not salvific in the afterlife according to many investigators. He then defines the five types of arising judgment (al-hukm al-hadith) that a person can have when judging something as true or false. He explains that the judge either has certainty or not. Certainty is further divided into two: (1) certainty with a cause (necessity or proof) which is called knowledge, and (2) certainty without cause which is called belief. Non-certainty is divided into three: (1) preponderant assumption (zann), (2) outweighed assumption (wahm—often translated as illusion), and (3) equal (shakk—doubt). Sanusi emphasizes the terminology: the first type of certainty is called knowledge, cognition, and certainty; the second is called belief. The first type of non-certainty is assumption, the second is illusion, and the third is doubt. This classification is foundational for evaluating the validity of faith later.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid)
اليقين (yaqin)
الجزم (jazm)
علم ('ilm)
اعتقاد (i'tiqad)
ظن (zann)
وهم (wahm)
شك (shakk)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All certainty is considered knowledge.
Correction: Sanusi distinguishes between certainty with a cause (knowledge) and certainty without a cause (belief), so not all certainty is knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, how many types of arising judgment are there in total?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Five
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Sanusi classifies judgments into knowledge, belief, assumption, doubt, and illusion, based on the presence and cause of certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have certainty in your beliefs about Allah, or are they merely inherited from your family? What would it take to move from belief (i'tiqad) to knowledge ('ilm) as Sanusi defines it—certainty based on proof? Will you seek that evidence today?
?
Sanusi warns that taqlid (blind following) is not salvific in the afterlife. Are you relying on the faith of your parents or scholars without personal conviction? What would it cost you to remain in that state if you are wrong?
?
You hold many opinions—some you are certain of, others you assume or doubt. Pick one belief you are currently uncertain about. Which of the five categories does it fall into? What step will you take to move it toward knowledge, or to honestly admit it is just a guess?

Scriptural Proofs for Nazar
Sanusi cites Quranic verses and ijma to prove obligation of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran and the consensus of the Companions establish the obligation of nazar and condemn taqlid.

Core Idea
The Quran commands reflection and warns delay; the Companions unanimously condemned blind imitation.

Sanusi begins by citing Quranic verses that command reflection and warn against delay. He then invokes the consensus of the Companions as further evidence. This lesson examines these scriptural and traditional proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Read the verses Sanusi quotes: 'Do they not reflect?' (7:184) and 'Indeed in the creation...' (2:164) and the warning in 7:185. Notice how they command reflection and warn against delay.
2
Understand the warning about death
Sanusi explains that Allah warns those who delay nazar because their death may come suddenly, causing them to die without faith.
3
Note the consensus argument
Sanusi adds that the Companions consistently condemned taqlid and warned against it, and this was a widespread saying without anyone denying it. This ijma is further evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلا : أَوَلَمْ يَتَفَكَّرُوا ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٨٤] ، وقوله سبحانَهُ : إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ... [البقرة : ١٦٤] الآيةَ ، وحَذَرَ سبحانه المتأنِّيَ (1) بالنظر بخوفِ قربِ موتِهِ ، فيفوتهُ النظرُ بتأنِّيهِ ، فيموتَ غير مؤمن فقال بعد قولِهِ : أَوَلَمْ يَنظُرُواْ فِي مَلَكُوتِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَمَا خَلَقَ اللَّهُ مِن شَيْءٍ وَأَنْ عَسَى أَن يَكُونَ قَدِ اقْتَرَبَ أَجَلُهُمْ ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٨٥] ، وإجماع الصحابة أيضاً دليل على وجوب النظرِ ؛ فإنَّها لم تزل تذمُّ التقليد وتُحذِّرُ منه ، وهو قول شائع بينهم مِنْ غير نكير
وقال القاضي رضي الله عنه : ( التقليد في علم التوحيد محال ؛ لأنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يُؤمَرَ بتقليدِ مَنْ شاءَ ، أو بتقليد المحقِّ ، والأمر بتقليدِ مَنْ شاءَ يلزم منه أن يكونَ مَنْ قَلَّدَ الكَفَرَةَ ممتثلاً ، وهو خلاف الإجماع ، وإِنْ أُمِرَ بتقليد المحقِّ : فإِمَّا أَنْ يُوْمَرَ بتقليد المحقِّ عندَ اللهِ تعالى وَإِنْ لم يَعلَمْ هو كونَهُ مُحِقاً ، أو بشرط علمِهِ بكونِهِ مُحِقاً ، والأَوَّلُ مِنْ تكليف المحالِ ، والثاني لا يعلمُ كونه محقاً إلا بعد النظر القويمِ ، وإذا نظر خرج عن كونِهِ مُقلّداً ، وإن قيلَ : يُؤْمَرُ بتقليدِ مَنْ غَلبَ على ظنه أنَّه على الحقِّ كما في الفروع .. لزم أن يكون كلُّ مَنْ قَلَّدَ مبتدعاً أو كافراً بناءً على رجحان قولِهِ في ظنّه ممتثلاً ، والإجماع على خلافه ((۲)
And He said: 'Do they not reflect?' [7:184], and His saying: 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth...' [2:164] the verse, and He warned the one who delays in reflection by fear of his death approaching, so reflection escapes him due to his delay, so he dies without faith, so He said after His saying: 'Do they not look into the kingdom of the heavens and the earth and whatever things Allah has created, and that perhaps their term has already drawn near?' [7:185]. And the consensus of the Companions is also evidence for the obligation of reflection; for they never ceased to condemn taqlid and to warn against it, and it is a widespread saying among them without any denial. And al-Qadi (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'Taqlid in the science of tawhid is impossible; because either one is commanded to imitate (taqlid) whomever he wishes, or to imitate the one who is truthful. The command to imitate whomever he wishes necessitates that the one who imitates the disbelievers would be obedient, which is contrary to consensus. And if he is commanded to imitate the truthful one, then either he is commanded to imitate the one who is truthful before Allah even if he himself does not know that he is truthful, or on the condition of his knowledge that he is truthful. The first is impossible (taklif al-muhal), and the second: he does not know him to be truthful except after correct reflection (nazar qawim), and if he reflects, he exits from being an imitator (muqallid). And if it is said: he is commanded to imitate the one who is preponderant in his assumption that he is upon the truth, as in subsidiary matters, then it would necessitate that everyone who imitates an innovator or disbeliever, based on the preponderance of his saying in his assumption, would be obedient, and consensus is contrary to that.'
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage provides two complementary proofs for the obligation of rational reflection (nazar) in theology and the prohibition of blind imitation (taqlid). The first proof is scriptural: the Quranic verses commanding reflection and warning those who delay, along with the consensus (ijma) of the Companions who consistently condemned taqlid. The second proof is a rational argument attributed to al-Qadi (al-Baqillani). He presents a logical dichotomy: either the law commands taqlid of anyone, or of the truthful person. The first leads to absurdity because it would validate imitating disbelievers. The second is analyzed further: if it means imitating one who is truthful in God's sight without the imitator knowing, that is impossible (taklif al-muhal). If it requires knowing the person is truthful, that knowledge itself requires nazar, which makes the person no longer a muqallid. Thus taqlid is impossible in Usul al-Din.

Key Terms
النظر (nazar)
التقليد (taqlid)
الإجماع (ijma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does Allah warn the one who delays nazar?
A) That he will forget.
B) That his death may approach and he will die without faith.
C) That he will be punished in this life.
D) That he must ask forgiveness.

Key Takeaway
The Quran commands reflection and warns against delay, and the Companions' consensus confirms the obligation of nazar and condemnation of taqlid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you putting off deep reflection on who your Lord is, assuming you have years to figure it out? If your term were to draw near tonight, would you die having truly reflected or merely imitated?
?
Think of one belief you hold about Allah. Did you inherit it from family or a teacher, or have you personally examined it using the intellect and revelation He gave you? What would it cost you to critically examine it today?
?
When you read the verse 'Do they not look into the kingdom of the heavens and the earth,' what stops you from actually doing that—real contemplation? Is it fear of finding something unsettling, or just a comfortable distraction? Ask yourself honestly, then commit to one concrete step this week.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Al-Qadi's Rational Proof Against Taqlid
Sanusi reproduces al-Qadi's rational proof that taqlid in tawhid is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi's logical argument that taqlid is impossible in matters of tawhid.

Core Idea
Taqlid in theology leads to logical absurdity; only nazar can identify the truthful.

After the scriptural proofs, Sanusi presents a rational argument attributed to al-Qadi (al-Baqillani). This argument uses a dichotomy to show that any command to blindly imitate either fails or requires nazar, making taqlid self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial dichotomy
Al-Qadi starts by dividing the possible command into two: either one is commanded to imitate anyone, or to imitate the truthful person. Note this basic division.
2
Explore the first branch
If commanded to imitate anyone, then imitating disbelievers would be obedience. This contradicts consensus. So this branch is invalid.
3
Explore the second branch
If commanded to imitate the truthful, there are two sub-branches: either without knowing who is truthful or with knowing. The first is impossible (taklif al-muhal); the second requires nazar to know who is truthful, which turns the person into a nazir, not a muqallid.
4
Consider the objection
If one says 'imitate based on preponderant assumption' as in fiqh, then the imitator of falsehood could be considered obedient. This too is against consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلا : أَوَلَمْ يَتَفَكَّرُوا ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٨٤] ، وقوله سبحانَهُ : إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ... [البقرة : ١٦٤] الآيةَ ، وحَذَرَ سبحانه المتأنِّيَ (1) بالنظر بخوفِ قربِ موتِهِ ، فيفوتهُ النظرُ بتأنِّيهِ ، فيموتَ غير مؤمن فقال بعد قولِهِ : أَوَلَمْ يَنظُرُواْ فِي مَلَكُوتِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَمَا خَلَقَ اللَّهُ مِن شَيْءٍ وَأَنْ عَسَى أَن يَكُونَ قَدِ اقْتَرَبَ أَجَلُهُمْ ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٨٥] ، وإجماع الصحابة أيضاً دليل على وجوب النظرِ ؛ فإنَّها لم تزل تذمُّ التقليد وتُحذِّرُ منه ، وهو قول شائع بينهم مِنْ غير نكير
وقال القاضي رضي الله عنه : ( التقليد في علم التوحيد محال ؛ لأنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يُؤمَرَ بتقليدِ مَنْ شاءَ ، أو بتقليد المحقِّ ، والأمر بتقليدِ مَنْ شاءَ يلزم منه أن يكونَ مَنْ قَلَّدَ الكَفَرَةَ ممتثلاً ، وهو خلاف الإجماع ، وإِنْ أُمِرَ بتقليد المحقِّ : فإِمَّا أَنْ يُوْمَرَ بتقليد المحقِّ عندَ اللهِ تعالى وَإِنْ لم يَعلَمْ هو كونَهُ مُحِقاً ، أو بشرط علمِهِ بكونِهِ مُحِقاً ، والأَوَّلُ مِنْ تكليف المحالِ ، والثاني لا يعلمُ كونه محقاً إلا بعد النظر القويمِ ، وإذا نظر خرج عن كونِهِ مُقلّداً ، وإن قيلَ : يُؤْمَرُ بتقليدِ مَنْ غَلبَ على ظنه أنَّه على الحقِّ كما في الفروع .. لزم أن يكون كلُّ مَنْ قَلَّدَ مبتدعاً أو كافراً بناءً على رجحان قولِهِ في ظنّه ممتثلاً ، والإجماع على خلافه ((۲)
And He said: 'Do they not reflect?' [7:184], and His saying: 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth...' [2:164] the verse, and He warned the one who delays in reflection by fear of his death approaching, so reflection escapes him due to his delay, so he dies without faith, so He said after His saying: 'Do they not look into the kingdom of the heavens and the earth and whatever things Allah has created, and that perhaps their term has already drawn near?' [7:185]. And the consensus of the Companions is also evidence for the obligation of reflection; for they never ceased to condemn taqlid and to warn against it, and it is a widespread saying among them without any denial. And al-Qadi (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'Taqlid in the science of tawhid is impossible; because either one is commanded to imitate (taqlid) whomever he wishes, or to imitate the one who is truthful. The command to imitate whomever he wishes necessitates that the one who imitates the disbelievers would be obedient, which is contrary to consensus. And if he is commanded to imitate the truthful one, then either he is commanded to imitate the one who is truthful before Allah even if he himself does not know that he is truthful, or on the condition of his knowledge that he is truthful. The first is impossible (taklif al-muhal), and the second: he does not know him to be truthful except after correct reflection (nazar qawim), and if he reflects, he exits from being an imitator (muqallid). And if it is said: he is commanded to imitate the one who is preponderant in his assumption that he is upon the truth, as in subsidiary matters, then it would necessitate that everyone who imitates an innovator or disbeliever, based on the preponderance of his saying in his assumption, would be obedient, and consensus is contrary to that.'
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage provides two complementary proofs for the obligation of rational reflection (nazar) in theology and the prohibition of blind imitation (taqlid). The first proof is scriptural: the Quranic verses commanding reflection and warning those who delay, along with the consensus (ijma) of the Companions who consistently condemned taqlid. The second proof is a rational argument attributed to al-Qadi (al-Baqillani). He presents a logical dichotomy: either the law commands taqlid of anyone, or of the truthful person. The first leads to absurdity because it would validate imitating disbelievers. The second is analyzed further: if it means imitating one who is truthful in God's sight without the imitator knowing, that is impossible (taklif al-muhal). If it requires knowing the person is truthful, that knowledge itself requires nazar, which makes the person no longer a muqallid. Thus taqlid is impossible in Usul al-Din.

Key Terms
التقليد (taqlid)
التوحيد (tawhid)
تكليف المحال (taklif al-muhal)
النظر القويم (nazar qawim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think taqlid is permissible in theology because it is allowed in fiqh.
Correction: Al-Qadi shows that in theology, any command to imitate based on preponderant assumption would validate imitating falsehood, which is against consensus.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Qadi argue that being commanded to imitate the truthful person on condition of knowing his truthfulness is impossible?
A) Because such knowledge requires nazar, which defeats taqlid.
B) Because the truthful person is unknown.
C) Because imitation is forbidden.
D) Because consensus prohibits it.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi demonstrates that any commanded taqlid in tawhid either leads to absurdity, impossibility, or self-defeating conditions, making nazar indispensable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were asked today to give a rational proof for your belief in Allah, could you do it—or have you simply inherited your faith from your environment? What does al-Qadi's argument say about the state of your iman?
?
When you face a doubt about your creed, do you immediately seek to verify it through reflection (nazar), or do you quickly dismiss it by repeating what you've been taught? How does that align with the requirement that tawhid must be based on nazar?
?
If taqlid in tawhid is truly impossible, then every moment you hold a belief without personally verifying its rational basis, you are falling short of the very foundation of faith. What is one specific belief you will now seek to verify through nazar, starting today?

Why Taqlid Cannot Equal Proof-Based Certainty
Sanusi refutes the claim that taqlid can be superior to nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why imitating others in matters of faith cannot produce the same level of certainty as rational inquiry based on proofs.

Core Idea
Certainty from blind imitation is never equal to certainty from proofs.

In this passage, Sanusi confronts a common but dangerous claim: that blindly following religious authorities can be superior to using your own reason. He dismantles this argument by showing that the certainty produced by taqlid is inherently flawed—it always leaves room for doubt. The source passage lays out the obligation of nazar and then delivers a crisp logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi builds his argument.
2
Identify the two types of certainty
Find where Sanusi contrasts 'certainty based on mere taqlid' with 'certainty produced by proofs'. Note their key difference: one admits the contrary, the other does not.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical force
Sanusi uses a rhetorical question ('So how can one claim its superiority?') and then an absurdity ('beyond the understanding of a rational person'). Consider why this makes the argument stronger.
This is not just logic; it's a persuasive style.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوبَ النظرِ وتحريم الاقتصار على التقليد ، وبعضهم يرى أن لا إيمان للمُقلد أصلاً (۱) ، فكيفَ يَدَّعي رجحانَهُ ؟! وأيضاً فمما لا يدخل تحت فهم عاقل : أنَّ الجزم المستند إلى مجرَّدِ التقليد - ومِنْ لازمِهِ قَبول احتمال النقيض - يكون مساوياً للجزم الذي أنتجته البراهينُ بحيثُ لا يحتمل النقيض بوجهِ مِنَ الوجوه .
The obligation of inquiry and the prohibition of restricting oneself to blind imitation, and some hold that there is no faith for the blind imitator at all. So how can one claim its superiority?! Also, it is beyond the understanding of a rational person that the certainty based on mere blind imitation - which necessarily admits the possibility of the contrary - should be equal to the certainty produced by proofs, which does not admit the contrary in any way whatsoever.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opposing view that considers blind imitation (taqlid) sufficient or even superior. Sanusi begins by stating the established position: rational inquiry (nazar) is obligatory, and restricting oneself to taqlid is forbidden. Some even hold that the muqallid has no faith at all. He then asks rhetorically: how can anyone claim that taqlid is superior? He provides an argument: the certainty derived from taqlid necessarily allows for the possibility of the opposite (i.e., it is not certain), whereas the certainty derived from definitive proofs does not admit any possibility of the contrary. It is absurd to equate the two. The structure is: claim (some say taqlid is superior) – refutation (rhetorical question) – argument (logical inequality).

Key Terms
النظر (nazar)
التقليد (taqlid)
الجزم (al-jazm)
البراهين (al-barahin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Blindly following a scholar produces the same level of certainty as deriving proofs oneself.
Correction: Sanusi shows that taqlid-based certainty always admits the possibility of the contrary, whereas proof-based certainty does not, so they are fundamentally different.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why cannot the certainty from taqlid equal the certainty from proofs?
A) Because proofs are longer and more detailed.
B) Because taqlid necessarily admits the possibility of the contrary, while proof does not.
C) Because taqlid is only for common people.
D) Because proofs are mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Blind imitation cannot produce the same unwavering certainty as reasoned proofs, so it cannot be superior to rational inquiry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a religious ruling or belief, do you ever stop to ask yourself: 'What is my basis for accepting this? Is it because I've heard it from a trusted source, or have I actually examined the proof myself?' What would this passage say about the certainty of your faith if it's only based on trust?
?
Think of one core belief you hold about Allah. Can you honestly say that your certainty about it is so strong that it does not admit any possibility of the contrary? If not, what does this lesson tell you about your obligation to seek proofs?
?
Are you content to rely on what you've been taught without personal reflection? This passage says that is 'beyond the understanding of a rational person.' How does that challenge your current approach to your own beliefs, and what step will you take today to move from taqlid to nazar?

Miraculous Knowledge and the Obligation of Inquiry
Sanusi answers the objection that knowledge may occur without nazar, limiting it to saints.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the claim that miraculous knowledge removes the duty of rational inquiry (nazar) and why the obligation remains for all except the exceptional saints.

Core Idea
Miraculous knowledge does not cancel the duty of inquiry for ordinary believers.

Sanusi now addresses a potential objection: if knowledge can be granted miraculously without inquiry, perhaps the obligation to engage in nazar is not universal. He interprets this objection as referring to rare saints and then explains that such exceptions do not nullify the general rule. The normal, divinely ordained path to knowledge is through effort, learning from scholars, and study.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Begin by reading the opening phrase under 'al-‘adah' (habit), which presents the objection: knowledge may be obtained without inquiry, breaking the usual pattern.
This objection challenges the necessity of nazar for everyone.
2
Identify Sanusi's interpretation
Notice that Sanusi interprets the objection as referring to some saints (awliya) who received knowledge miraculously. He then clarifies that such a person is not a muqallid (blind imitator), because what they obtain is certain knowledge, not mere belief.
This distinction is key: the person is like an inquirer in terms of certainty.
3
Understand the general rule
Sanusi states that the dependence of theoretical knowledge on inquiry is only by habit; Allah can make it necessary if He wills. However, this miraculous possibility does not remove the obligation of nazar for those who have not reached that station. The normal means prescribed by the Law is effort and learning.
The obligation of nazar remains for everyone who is not a recipient of such a miracle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العادة
[ قد تحصلُ المعرفة مِنْ غير نظر خرقاً للعادة ]
ولعله أرادَ بعضَ مَنْ لم ينظرْ مِنْ أولياءِ اللهِ ، وخُرِقَتْ في حقه ووُهِبَ لَهُ مِنَ المعارفِ ما لا يُتوصَّلُ إليه بالنظر ، حتى صارَتْ علوم الناظرينَ بالنسبة إلى ما أُعطِيَ مِنَ العلوم كلا شيء ، وإذا أرادَ هذا فليس هو مِنْ محلَّ النزاع ؛ لأنَّ نزاعنا في المقلد ، وهذا الذي ذكر ليس بمقلّد ، بل هو كالناظر في أنَّ الحاصل له علم لا اعتقاد ، وتوقُفُ العلم غير الضروري على النظرِ إِنَّما هو بحسَبِ العادةِ ، ويجوزُ في قدرة الله تعالى أن يجعلَ العلوم النظريَّةَ لِمَنْ شَاءَ ضروريةً ؛ بحيثُ لا يُفتقَرُ في تحصيلها إلى نظر ، إلا أنَّ تجويز مثل هذا الخارقِ الذي لم يُعْطَ إلا للنادر مِنَ الأولياء .. لا يُسقط وجوب النظر في حقٌّ مَنْ لم يَحصُلْ له هذا المَقامُ ، والذي جرَتْ بهِ العادةُ وأمرَ بهِ الشرعُ تحصيل العلومِ مِنْ طريقها المألوف ؛ وهو الاجتهاد في النظرِ ، والتعلُّمُ مِنَ العلماء ، والتزام التعب في الدرس ، والرحلة في طلب الفوائدِ
Habit: [Knowledge may be obtained from without inquiry, breaking the habit.] Perhaps he meant some of the saints of Allah who did not engage in inquiry, and a miracle was wrought in their favor, and they were granted such knowledge as cannot be attained by inquiry, so that the sciences of the inquirers become as nothing compared to what they were given. If he meant this, then this is not the subject of dispute; because our dispute is about the blind imitator, and the one mentioned is not a blind imitator; rather, he is like the inquirer in that what he obtains is knowledge, not belief. And the dependence of non-necessary knowledge on inquiry is only according to habit, and it is possible in the power of Allah the Exalted to make theoretical sciences necessary for whomever He wills, so that they do not need inquiry to acquire them. However, the possibility of such a miracle, which is only given to the rare among the saints, does not remove the obligation of inquiry for one who has not attained this station. And the customary way, which the Law commands, is to acquire sciences through their usual path: that is, striving in inquiry, learning from scholars, enduring hardship in study, and traveling in search of benefits.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection (set off by the heading 'al-‘adah' and the bracketed text) states that knowledge may be obtained without inquiry, breaking the usual habit. Sanusi interprets this as referring to some saints (awliya) who were granted knowledge miraculously. He then answers: such a person is not a muqallid, but rather like an inquirer because he has certain knowledge, not mere belief. Therefore, this case does not fall under our dispute. He then explains the general rule: the dependence of non-necessary (theoretical) knowledge on inquiry is only by habit; Allah can make such knowledge necessary (intuitive) for whomever He wills. However, the possibility of such a rare miracle does not remove the obligation of inquiry for those who have not reached that station. The normal way, sanctioned by the Law(Shari‘a), is to acquire knowledge through its usual means: effort in inquiry, learning from scholars, enduring study, and traveling for knowledge.

Key Terms
النظر (nazar)
التقليد (taqlid)
خرق العادة (khariq al-‘adah)
الأولياء (al-awliya)
العلم الضروري (al-‘ilm al-daruri)
العلم النظري (al-‘ilm al-nazari)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the occurrence of miraculous knowledge removes the obligation of nazar for everyone.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that miraculous knowledge is granted only to exceptional saints and does not apply to the ordinary muqallid; thus the obligation of nazar remains for others.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi say that the person who receives knowledge miraculously is not a muqallid?
A) Because he is a saint, and saints cannot be muqallids.
B) Because he obtains certain knowledge, not mere belief, similar to an inquirer.
C) Because he never engages in taqlid.
D) Because his knowledge is necessary, not theoretical.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of rational inquiry (nazar) remains for all who have not attained the station of miraculous knowledge, as it is the normal means prescribed by the Law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you relying on the hope of divine intervention to grant you knowledge without effort, rather than committing to the difficult path of nazar? What step will you take today to begin that path?
?
The passage says the customary and commanded way is to strive in inquiry and learn from scholars. Is your current approach to learning your religion characterized by diligent study and seeking scholars, or are you passively consuming information? What will change?
?
Sanusi distinguishes the saint who receives knowledge without inquiry from the ordinary muqallid. Which category describes you—one who has attained certainty through inquiry, or one who follows without evidence? If the latter, are you content with that?

The Obligation of Proof: Fard 'Ayn vs Fard Kifayah
Sanusi distinguishes fard 'ayn and fard kifayah regarding deep investigation of doubts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between fard 'ayn and fard kifayah regarding the duty to seek proofs for articles of faith.

Core Idea
Not everyone must master deep proofs; basic evidence for each creedal point suffices as an individual duty.

Sanusi addresses a potential objection about the difficulty of investigating subtle doubts. He concedes that for some people, deep investigation is not only unnecessary but could be harmful. However, he then clarifies the real obligation: every believer must know the basics of faith with proof, but the advanced study of doubts is a collective responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if this speaker meant... then he is near.'
Pay attention to the words 'فروض الأعيان' and 'فروض الكفاية'.
2
Identify the two obligations
Locate where Sanusi distinguishes between fard 'ayn (individual obligation) and fard kifayah (collective obligation). Understand the difference.
The individual obligation is 'to know every article of faith with some proof'.
3
Reflect on the rationale
Think about why deep investigation might be forbidden for some people. What is the risk? How does this show mercy in the religion?
Sanusi mentions fear of doubts becoming fixed in the soul.
4
Apply the principle
Consider how this principle applies today: what level of proof is required for an average Muslim? When is advanced study needed?
Advanced study is for those with the capacity and intention to benefit the community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم؛ لو أراد هذا القائلُ أنَّ النظر في دقائقِ الشُّبَهِ التي لا يُتخلَّص منها إلا بغوص عظيم يحرمُ على مَنْ هو بليد الطبع جامد القريحة ؛ بحيث يُخشى أنْ يَرسَخَ منها شيء في نفسِهِ ويَعجِزَ عَن دفعِهِ . . لَقَرُبَ ؛ إِذْ ليسَ ذلكَ مِنْ فروض الأعيانِ عندَنا ، بل هو مِنْ فروض الكفاية وإنَّما فرضُ العين في حقٌّ كلِّ مُكلَّفٍ : أن يعرف كلَّ عَقْدِ مِنْ عقودِ الإيمان ببرهان ما ، وذلكَ سهل على مَنْ وُفِّقَ .
Yes; if this speaker meant that examining the intricacies of doubts which cannot be escaped except by great depth is forbidden for one who is dull-natured and rigid in intellect, such that it is feared that something of them may become fixed in his soul and he is unable to repel it... then he is near [the truth]; for that is not among the individual obligations according to us, but rather it is among the collective obligations. Rather, the individual obligation upon every legally responsible person is to know every article of faith with some proof, and that is easy for one who is granted success.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of the types of obligation regarding the acquisition of proofs. It begins with a concession: if a critic meant that delving into the intricacies of doubts (daqaiq al-shubah) that can only be escaped by great depth is forbidden for a person of dull nature and rigid intellect, such that he might become infected by doubts and cannot repel them, then the critic is close to correct. However, Sanusi immediately distinguishes: such deep investigation is not a personal obligation (fard 'ayn) but a collective obligation (fard kifayah)—some qualified individuals must undertake it, but not everyone. The real personal obligation on every responsible person (mukallaf) is to know each article of faith with some proof (bi-burhan ma), meaning any valid proof, not necessarily the most refined. Sanusi adds that this is easy for one who is granted divine success (tawfiq). The nuance here is that while profound theological study is not required of all, basic evidentiary knowledge of the creed is individually incumbent.

Key Terms
فروض الأعيان
فروض الكفاية
برهان ما
عقود الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That every believer must master complex philosophical arguments to have valid faith.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the individual obligation is only to know each article of faith with some proof, not necessarily deep or complex proofs. In-depth study is a collective duty for those capable.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what type of obligation is examining the intricacies of doubts (daqaiq al-shubah) for a person of dull nature?
A) It is a personal obligation (fard 'ayn)
B) It is a collective obligation (fard kifayah)
C) It is forbidden (haram)
D) It is recommended (mandub)

Key Takeaway
Every Muslim must know the basics of faith with proof, but advanced theological investigation is only a collective duty for those qualified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever stopped to examine the proof behind just one article of your faith? If not, what does that say about your sense of obligation before Allah today?
?
Are you one who pursues intellectual doubts beyond your capacity, risking confusion—or do you content yourself with the basic proofs Sanusi deems sufficient? Which path will you choose starting today?
?
You know now that every mukallaf must know each creedal point with some proof. What will you do to ensure you are fulfilling that personal duty before you meet your Lord?

The Danger of Taqlid: From the Heart to the Grave
Sanusi explains how taqlid causes hypocrisy and leads to punishment in the grave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taqlid in matters of faith leads to hypocrisy and how that manifests in the grave.

Core Idea
Blind faith without proof leaves the heart diseased and leads to punishment in the grave.

Sanusi continues his critique of taqlid by showing its dire consequences. He argues that even if taqlid were valid, the heart remains sick and confused, making the person like a hypocrite. This is dramatically illustrated by the hadith about the questioning in the grave.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of Sanusi's argument.
Pay attention to the pronoun reference at the beginning.
2
Understand the link between taqlid and hypocrisy
Notice how Sanusi explains that even if taqlid were valid, the heart remains sick and confused, leading to hypocrisy. The Quranic verse confirms this.
Reflect on why the heart's sickness leads to failure in the grave.
3
Study the hadith on grave punishment
Read the hadith narrated by Ibn Dihaq. Note the response of the hypocrite and the angels' reply. Understand the severity of the punishment.
The phrase 'I don't know' is the key.
4
Reflect on the description of the angels
Consider the vivid description of the two angels. How does this imagery reinforce the seriousness of taqlid?
Link this back to the state of the hypocrite's heart.

يعني : أنَّ الضميرَ في ( صاحبها ( يعود على حرفة التقليد ؛ التصميم على العقائدِ بغيرِ تحصينها بالدليل لا يأمن صاحبها ـ على تقدير صحة القولِ بالتقليدِ - مِنْ زوالِهِ عندَ عروض أدنى شبهة وعلى تقديرِ أنْ يقابلَ ذلكَ ويكابر نفسَهُ بالتصميم اللساني . .. فأَنَّى ينفعُهُ ذلك والقلبُ الذي هو محلُّ الإيمانِ مريض مُتحيّر يقولُ : لا أدري ؟! فيدخلُ في زمرة المنافقين الذين تخالف ألسنتهم قلوبهم قال الله تعالى في حقهم : ﴿ فِي قُلُوبِهِم مَّرَضٌ فَزَادَهُمُ اللَّهُ مَرَضًا ﴾ [البقرة : ١٠] أي : لمَّا مرضَتْ قلوبهم لم ينتفعوا بما في ألسنتهم ، وهذا المريضُ القلبِ المرتاب هو مِنَ القائلين في القبر عند مساءلة الملكين : لا أدري ، سمعتُ الناسَ يقولونَ شيئاً فقلتُه ، إذ هذا حال قلبه في حياتِهِ وعندَ موتِهِ ، واللسانُ في ذلك الموطن لا يتركُ - كما في الدنيا ـ أنْ يَتشَبَّعَ بما ليس في القلبِ

قال ابنُ دهاق رحمهُ الله ورضي عنه في ( شرح الإرشادِ » لما تكلَّمَ وَأَمَّا على فتنة الملكين في القبرِ ، وساق الحديث ، وفي آخره : « الْمُنَافِقُ أَوِ الْمُرْتَابُ فَيَقُولُ : لَا أَدْرِي ، سَمِعْتُ النَّاسَ يَقُولُونَ شَيْئاً فَقُلْتُهُ ، فَيَقُولَانِ لَهُ : لَا دَرَيْتَ وَلَا تَلَيْتَ ، وَيَضْرِبَانِهِ بِالْمِقْمَعِ مِنَ الْحَدِيدِ ، فَيَصِيحُ صَيْحَةٌ يَسْمَعُهَا كُلُّ شَيْءٍ إِلَّا الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنْسَ » ، وفي حديث : « إِلَّا الثَّقَلَيْنِ ؛ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنْسَ » ، وفي الحديثِ المشتمل على عذاب القبرِ في وصفِ الملكينِ : أنَّهما أسودان أزرقانِ ، يَنْحِتَانِ الأرضَ بأنيابهما ، ويطأ ان شعورهما ، وأعينهما كالبرق الخاطف ، وأصواتُهما كالرعدِ القاصف . .
Meaning: That the pronoun in 'its possessor' refers to the craft of taqlid. The determination upon beliefs without fortifying them with proof does not make its possessor safe -- on the assumption of the validity of taqlid -- from its vanishing when the slightest doubt arises. And on the assumption that he opposes that and forces himself with verbal determination... So how does that benefit him when the heart, which is the place of faith, is sick, confused, saying: 'I don't know'?! So he enters the group of hypocrites whose tongues contradict their hearts. Allah the Exalted said concerning them: {In their hearts is disease, and Allah has increased their disease} [Al-Baqarah: 10], meaning: When their hearts became diseased, they did not benefit from what is on their tongues. And this sick, doubtful heart is among those who say in the grave when questioned by the two angels: 'I don't know; I heard people saying something and I said it.' For such is the state of his heart in his life and at his death, and the tongue in that situation does not refrain -- as in this world -- from being satiated with what is not in the heart. Ibn Dihaq (may Allah have mercy on him and be pleased with him) said in his commentary on al-Irshad, when he spoke about the trial of the two angels in the grave: He narrated the hadith, at the end of which: 'The hypocrite or the doubter says: 'I don't know; I heard people saying something and I said it.' So they say to him: 'May you not know and may you not learn!' And they strike him with iron maces, and he screams a scream that everything hears except jinn and humans.' And in another hadith: 'Except the two heavy ones: jinn and humans.' And in the hadith that includes the punishment of the grave, describing the two angels: They are black and blue, they dig the earth with their fangs, and they trample their hair, and their eyes are like lightning flashing, and their voices like thunder crashing.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is an argument against taqlid (blind following) by demonstrating its dangerous consequences. The author begins by explaining that the pronoun 'its possessor' refers to the one who practices taqlid. The person who holds beliefs without having fortified them with proof is never safe, even if one assumes taqlid is valid, because his beliefs can vanish when any doubt arises. Even if he forces himself to verbally assert those beliefs, it does not help because his heart—the seat of faith—remains sick and confused, saying 'I don't know.' Such a person joins the hypocrites whose tongues contradict their hearts, as Allah says in Surah Al-Baqarah verse 10: their hearts are diseased, so they gain no benefit from their verbal statements. This person, with a sick and doubting heart, is among those who, when questioned in the grave by the two angels, will say, 'I don't know; I heard people saying something and I said it.' This is the condition of his heart both in life and at death; his tongue, as in this world, tries to be satisfied with what is not in the heart. The author then quotes Ibn Dihaq, who in his commentary on al-Irshad narrated a hadith about the trial of the grave: The hypocrite or doubter will say he merely heard people say something and repeated it. The angels will rebuke him and strike him with iron maces, causing a scream heard by all except jinn and humans. Another hadith adds that the scream is heard by everything except the two heavy ones (jinn and humans). The author then gives a vivid description of the two angels: black and blue, digging the earth with their fangs, trampling their own hair, with eyes like lightning and voices like thunder. Thus, the passage powerfully links taqlid to the dire fate of hypocrisy and punishment in the grave.

Misconception: Some think taqlid in foundational beliefs is acceptable and safe.
Correction: The passage shows that taqlid leads to a sick heart, hypocrisy, and punishment in the grave, so it is not acceptable.

Checkpoint

A) I believe in Allah and the Messenger.
B) I don't know; I heard people saying something and I said it.
C) I have proof for my faith.
D) I was sincere in my worship.

Key Takeaway
Taqlid leaves the heart diseased and leads to hypocrisy, resulting in punishment in the grave when the person can only say 'I don't know'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada or affirm Islamic beliefs, is there any hidden 'I don't know' in your heart about a single article of faith? If so, what concrete step will you take today to replace that doubt with certainty through study and reflection?
?
The taqlid practitioner in this passage verbally insists on faith but his heart is sick. Think of one belief you hold simply because you were raised in a Muslim family. Are you willing to face the two angels with the excuse 'I heard people saying it'? What proof can you now acquire to make that belief your own?
?
Sanusi warns that the tongue in the grave cannot be 'satiated with what is not in the heart.' Where in your life is there a gap between what you say and what you truly believe inwardly? What is the first step you will take to align your heart with your words, using evidence from revelation and reason?

Strong Conviction and Worship Do Not Prove Truth
Sanusi refutes the claim that strong conviction and worship indicate truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why strong conviction and abundant worship are not reliable indicators of true belief, and why rational proof is necessary to distinguish truth from falsehood.

Core Idea
Strong faith and devotion are not evidence of truth—they can exist in false religions too.

We often think that someone’s firm belief and sincere worship prove they are on the right path. But Sanusi challenges this assumption. In this lesson, he shows that such characteristics are common among followers of false religions, and that only correct rational consideration can distinguish truth from falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get the full meaning.
Notice how Sanusi directly states his thesis at the start.
2
Identify Sanusi's main claim
Find the sentence that states the thesis: strength of determination and worship do not conclusively indicate knowledge.
This is the central point being argued.
3
Understand the refutation by counterexample
Sanusi points to Jews, Christians, and idolaters who also have strong determination and worship. Consider why this undermines the claim that these traits prove truth.
These groups are used as a refutation (naqd).
4
Grasp the cause of conviction
Read the explanation: determination arises from upbringing and association, not necessarily from truth. Note the concept of compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab).
Upbringing can produce strong certainty even in falsehood.
5
Conclude the necessity of rational proof
Sanusi concludes that since certainty does not entail truth, one must use correct rational proofs (nazar) to have insight. This is the goal of kalam.
This connects to the broader purpose of the discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوة التصميم وكثرة العبادة لا يقطعان بالمعرفة
ولا يغتر المقلّد ويستدلُّ أَنَّهُ على الحقِّ بِقُوَّةِ تصميمِهِ وكثرة تعبُّدِهِ ؛ للنقض عليهِ بتصميم اليهود والنصارى وعبدة الأوثانِ ومَنْ في معناهم ؛ تقليداً لأحبارهم وآبائهم الضالين المضلين
يعني : أنَّ تصميم المقلّدِ على الحقِّ وعدم رجوعِهِ عنه ولو نُشِرَ بالمناشر ، وكثرة عباداتِهِ .. لا يدلُّ أَنَّهُ على بصيرةٍ مِنْ دِينِهِ ، ، إِذْ ليسَ جزمه وتصميمه على الحقِّ مِنْ حيثُ كونه حقاً ، بل مِنْ حيث نشأته بينَ قوم يقولونَ ذلكَ ، والنشأة والمخالطة لهما أثر عظيم في التصميم حقاً كانَ المُصمَّمُ عليهِ أو باطلاً ؛ بدليل أنَّ مثل هذا التصميمِ يُوجَدُ كثيراً في ذوي الجهل المركَبِ ؛ كعامَّةِ اليهود والنصارى ونحوهم ، وإذا كانَ مُجرَّد الوهم الكاذب لهُ أثر في التصميم.. فما بالك بما فوقه ؟! ولهذا قالوا : مَنْ جزمَ في قلبِهِ بالحقِّ ولم يدرِ لذلك سبباً خاصاً يرجعُ إليه . . فهو مُقلَدٌ لا بصيرة لهُ حقاً ، فإذاً ؛ لا ملازمة بين الجزم الاعتقادي وكون المجزومِ بهِ وإذا انتفَتْ بينهما الملازمةُ وجبَ أنْ يأتي بما بينه وبينَ الحَقِّ ملازمةٌ ليميز ما هو عليهِ مِنَ الدينِ ؛ أهوَ مِنَ الحقِّ أم مِنَ الباطل ؟ ليكونَ على بصيرة في دينِهِ ، وليس ذلك إلا بالنظر الصحيح في البراهين ، فتعيَّنَ النظرُ، وهو المطلوب .
Strength of determination and abundance of worship do not conclusively indicate knowledge. And let not the blind follower be deceived and infer that he is upon the truth because of the strength of his determination and abundance of his worship; for refutation of that is provided by the determination of the Jews, Christians, idolaters, and those like them, in imitation of their rabbis and forefathers who are astray and mislead. He means: that the determination of the blind follower upon the truth and his not desisting from it even if he were sawn with saws, and his abundant worship, do not indicate that he is upon insight into his religion, because his certainty and determination are not upon the truth from the perspective of it being truth, but rather from the perspective of his upbringing among a people who say that. Upbringing and association have a great effect on determination, whether the object of determination is true or false; evidence for this is that such determination is found frequently among those with compound ignorance, like the common Jews, Christians, and others like them. And if mere false illusion has an effect on determination, then what about what is above it?! For this reason they said: Whoever is certain in his heart about the truth but does not know a specific reason to which he can refer for that, then he is a blind follower, truly having no insight. So then there is no necessary correlation between belief-certainty and the truth of the object of belief; and when the correlation between them is negated, he must bring something that has a correlation with the truth to distinguish what he is upon of religion—whether it is from the truth or from falsehood—in order to have insight into his religion. And that is only through correct consideration of proofs, so consideration is necessitated, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the argument that strong conviction and abundant worship are proof that one is on the truth. Sanusi begins by stating that strength of determination and worship do not conclusively indicate knowledge. He warns the blind follower (muqallid) not to be deceived into thinking that his own firmness and devotion prove he is right, because the same characteristics are found among Jews, Christians, idolaters, and others, who are merely imitating their misguided leaders. He then explains the reasoning: the follower's determination arises not from the truth itself but from his upbringing among people who hold that belief. Upbringing and association strongly influence conviction, whether the belief is true or false. Evidence is that such determination is common among those with compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab), i.e., those who are ignorant and unaware of their ignorance, like ordinary Jews and Christians. If even false illusion can produce strong determination, then surely stronger influences can too. Therefore, the scholars said that anyone who is certain in his heart about the truth without knowing a specific rational reason for it is a mere blind follower without true insight. Hence there is no necessary correlation (mulazama) between the subjective certainty and the objective truth of the belief. Since this correlation is absent, one must bring something that does have a correlation with truth—namely, correct rational proof (nazar)—to distinguish true religion from false. Therefore, rational consideration is necessitated, and that is the goal.

Key Terms
تصميم
المقلّد
الجهل المركب
ملازمة
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If someone is very certain and devout, their religion must be true.
Correction: Sanusi shows that false religions also have these features, so certainty and devotion are not proof of truth.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does strong determination and worship not prove that one is upon the truth?
A) Because such determination is only found in false religions.
B) Because determination can arise from upbringing and association, regardless of truth or falsehood.
C) Because worship is not a valid act without knowledge.
D) Because the Jew and Christian do not worship at all.

Key Takeaway
Strong conviction and abundant worship do not guarantee truth; only correct rational proof can do that.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any belief you hold with absolute certainty that you cannot defend with a specific rational proof? If so, how is your certainty different from that of the Jews and Christians described by Sanusi?
?
Your acts of worship may be abundant and sincere, but could they be mere imitation of your upbringing? What would it take for you to be certain that your worship is based on genuine insight rather than habit?
?
Sanusi says that the blind follower has no insight even if he is sawed with saws. Are you prepared to examine the basis of your faith, even if it feels unsettling? What is the cost of not doing so?

Refuting Inspiration as the Sole Path to Knowledge
Sanusi refutes the Indian claim that inspiration alone suffices for knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that mere removal of bodily distractions is insufficient for knowledge, and why prior sciences and reflection (nazar and tajrid) are necessary.

Core Idea
Inspiration without prior knowledge and reflection cannot produce true knowledge.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a claim attributed to some Indian philosophers: that the soul, when freed from bodily distractions, can directly attain knowledge through inspiration. He refutes this by showing that even after removing distractions, one still needs prior sciences and the process of reflection to achieve specific knowledge. This lesson breaks down the refutation and its logical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the literal English translation. Note the key terms.
The passage is a refutation, so pay attention to the claim and the counter-argument.
2
Identify the Indian claim
The claim is that when the soul abstracts itself from bodily concerns, it perceives things directly. They believe the soul is inherently prepared to receive knowledge.
This is the position Sanusi refutes.
3
Understand the refutation
Sanusi says that removing distractions alone is not enough. The soul still needs prior sciences—either necessary or acquired—and the process of reflection (nazar) and abstraction (tajrid) to arrive at the specific sought knowledge.
The refutation highlights that inspiration is not self-sufficient; it depends on prior cognitive tools.
4
Reflect on the role of nazar and tajrid
Nazar (reflection) and tajrid (abstraction) are presented as necessary consequences for achieving knowledge. Think about how these relate to the removal of distractions.
This shows that even in spiritual pursuits, intellectual effort is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَنْ قالَ : إنَّ طريق المعرفة الإلهام ومَنْ قالَ مِنَ الهنودِ : إِنَّ طريق المعرفة الإلهام ، وعَنَوا بهِ : أَنَّ النفس إذا تَجرَّدَتْ للشيء وأزالَتِ الشواغل البدنية أدركَتْه ؛ فإنَّها في أصل خلقتِها مُستعِدَّةٌ لقَبولِ المعارفِ .. فالردُّ عليهم : أَنَّ مُجرَّدَ إزالة الشواغل لا يُحصِّلُ المطلوب الخاص إلا مع حضور علوم ؛ إمَّا ضرورية أو غير ضروريةٍ يَترتّب عليها المطلوب ؛ وهو النظر والتجريد لازمه
Whoever said: 'The path to knowledge is inspiration.' And some of the Indians said: 'The path to knowledge is inspiration,' meaning by it that the soul, when it abstracts itself for something and removes the bodily distractions, perceives it; for in its original constitution it is prepared to accept knowledges. So the refutation against them is that mere removal of distractions does not achieve the specific sought except with the presence of sciences, either necessary or non-necessary, upon which the sought depends; and that is reflection (nazar) and abstraction (tajrid) are its necessary consequences.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim made by some Indians that the path to knowledge is ilham (inspiration). They argued that the soul, when it abstracts itself from bodily concerns, is able to directly perceive reality because it is inherently prepared. The author refutes this by stating that merely removing distractions is insufficient; one must also have prior sciences (either necessary or acquired) to achieve the specific knowledge sought. The tools of reflection and abstraction are necessary for this process.

نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge can be attained solely through inspiration by removing bodily distractions.
Correction: The author argues that even after removing distractions, one needs prior sciences and the processes of reflection (nazar) and abstraction (tajrid) to achieve knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw the author identifies in the Indian claim?
A) They deny the existence of knowledge.
B) They think inspiration alone suffices without prior sciences.
C) They rely on bodily practices.
D) They reject reflection.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge requires both prior sciences and active reflection, not just passive inspiration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Two Interpretations of Divine Knowledge by Necessity
Sanusi analyzes the two interpretations of the claim that God is known by necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible meanings of the claim that Allah is known by necessity of the intellect and why one is accepted while the other is rejected.

Core Idea
Not all claims of necessary knowledge are the same—one is sound, one is false.

Sanusi addresses a claim from some scholars that knowledge of God is necessary. He carefully distinguishes between two interpretations: one that is accepted and one that is clearly false. This distinction is crucial for understanding the role of reflection and proof in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on two interpretations
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to see the two interpretations and Sanusi's response to each.
2
Identify the first interpretation
Notice that the first interpretation is about the process of reflection ending in necessary premises. Sanusi accepts this.
This is about the foundation of proofs.
3
Identify the second interpretation
The second interpretation claims knowledge of God is innate without any need for reflection. Sanusi calls this invalid.
This is a common misunderstanding.
4
Understand why the first is accepted
Sanusi explains that all proofs must end in necessary premises to avoid infinite regress. This is a key principle of reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما نُقِلَ عن طائفةٍ مِنْ أهل العلم : أنَّ اللهَ معروفٌ بضرورةِ العقل ... إلى آخره : فإن أرادوا أنَّ النظر في معرفتِهِ تعالى ينتهي إلى الضرورة.. فمُسلَّمٌ ؛ لأنَّ معرفته جلَّ وعلا بل ومعرفة جميع عقائدِ الإيمانِ إنَّما هو بالبراهين ، والبراهينُ لا بدَّ أنْ تنتهي إلى مُقدِّماتٍ ضرورية ، وإلا لزمَ التسلسل ، ولم تنتج القطع الذي كُلِّفْنا بهِ في العقائدِ وإنْ أرادوا أنَّهُ معروفٌ بضرورة العقل بَدْءاً بحيثُ لا يفتقر إلى نظر أصلاً . . فلا خفاء في بطلان هذه المقالة وقد اختلف الأئمَّةُ بعد تحقيق المسألة حدوث العالم ببرهانه : هل دلالته بعد على وجودِ محدثِهِ ضرورية وإليه ذهب الفخرُ ، أم نظريةٌ يُحتاجُ معها إلى ضميمة شيءٍ آخر ، وإليه ذهب إمام الحرمين وجماعة مِنَ المُحقِّقِينَ ؟ على ما سيأتي تحقيقُهُ إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ تعالى (۱) ، فإِذا كانَ هذا الخلافُ بعد علمِ الحدوثِ للعالم المتضِحِ بحسَبِ الظاهرِ دلالتُهُ العقائد ؛ وهو علم وجودِهِ جلَّ وعلا الذي اتفقت عليه جميعُ العقلاء إلا مَنْ لا يُعتَدُّ بهِ ، وَلَئِن سَأَلْتَهُم مَّنْ خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ في أظهرِ
And as for what is transmitted from a group of scholars that Allah is known by necessity of the intellect... etc.: If they mean that reflection on knowing Him Most High ends in necessity, then it is accepted; because knowledge of Him, Exalted and High, indeed knowledge of all tenets of faith, is only through proofs, and proofs must end in necessary premises, otherwise there would be infinite regress, and they would not produce the certainty which we are obligated to have in the doctrines. And if they mean that He is known by necessity of the intellect from the outset such that it does not require any reflection at all, then there is no hiddenness in the invalidity of this statement. And the imams have differed after establishing the matter of the world's contingency through its proof: whether its indication of the existence of its Maker is necessary – and this is the view of al-Fakhr (al-Razi) – or theoretical, needing an additional element – and this is the view of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) and a group of the investigators? This will be clarified later, God willing. So when this disagreement occurs after knowledge of the world's contingency, which is evident in its indication of the doctrines – and it is the knowledge of His existence, Exalted and High, which all rational beings agree upon except those who are not taken into account – and if you ask them, 'Who created the heavens and the earth?' in the most apparent...
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage deals with the claim that knowledge of God is necessary (daruri). Sanusi presents two interpretations: first, if they mean that the process of reflection ends in necessary premises (i.e., the starting points of reasoning are self-evident), then this is accepted because all proofs must ultimately rest on necessary premises to avoid infinite regress (tasalsul). Second, if they mean that knowledge of God is innate and does not require any reflection at all, then this is false. Then Sanusi mentions a disagreement among scholars after establishing the proof of the world's contingency (huduth al-‘alam): does the proof of contingency directly yield knowledge of a Maker necessarily (as held by al-Fakhr al-Razi), or does it require an additional premise (such as possibility/ imkan) to reach that conclusion (as held by Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni and others)? He says this will be clarified later. Then he points out that this disagreement occurs even after the contingency of the world is established, and that the existence of God is agreed upon by all rational people except a few. The passage ends with a partial quote from the Quran: 'And if you ask them, Who created the heavens and the earth?' implying that even polytheists acknowledge this, showing that the point of disagreement is about the method of inference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that knowledge of God is purely innate and requires no thought or reflection.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that while the foundations of proof are necessary, the knowledge of God itself requires reflection on those proofs.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi accept as true about knowing God by necessity?
A) It is innate without reflection.
B) Reflection ends in necessary premises.
C) No proof is needed.
D) It is theoretical.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi distinguishes between accepted necessity of reflection ending in premises and false necessity of innate knowledge without reflection.

?

?
The lesson shows that reflection is necessary to reach certainty. When you face doubts about Allah's existence or attributes, do you actively seek out evidence and reasoning, or do you avoid the discomfort of reflection? What step will you take today to engage with the proofs?
?
Sanusi warns that claiming innate knowledge without reflection leads to an invalid position. Is there any area of your faith where you have passively accepted what you were taught without personally verifying it through reflection? What can you do to change that starting now?
